				<div class="page">
		<h1 class="content-title">The Toobin principle</h1>

		<article class="post-4105 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-uncategorized" id="post-4105">
		
			<div class="datebox">
				<span class="month">Aug.</span>
				</br>
				<span class="day">6</span>
			</div>
		
<!--			<p class="post-author">August 6th, 2013</p> -->

			<div class="entry">
				<h2>Repeal the concept of an informed public, repress your decision to take such a drastic step. But it&#8217;s not just Jeffrey Toobin. Congress did it too.</h2>
<p>Last week <a href="http://www.mediaite.com/tv/greenwald-and-nyts-james-risen-completely-deflate-jeffrey-toobins-tortured-logic-on-nsa-snowden/">on his CNN program</a> Piers Morgan had just about finished a little speech on how you can&#8217;t have any bloke with a security clearance spewing classified information &#8220;on a whim&#8221; when James Risen, national security reporter for the New York Times, interrupted him: <em>which document that&#8217;s come out don&#8217;t you want to talk about?</em> Meaning: which of the things we&#8217;ve learned from Edward Snowden would you, as a journalist, prefer not to know? Which part of the surveillance story that&#8217;s come to light should have remained in darkness?</p>
<p>It was a good question. Piers Morgan did not have much of a reply.</p>
<p>When, on the same program, Jeffrey Toobin of the New Yorker said that public discussion about previously classified materials was &#8220;a good thing&#8221; but he still thought Edward Snowden was a criminal and shouldn&#8217;t have done what he did, Risen interrupted: &#8220;We wouldn&#8217;t be having this discussion if it wasn&#8217;t for him,&#8221; he said. &#8220;That’s the thing I don’t understand about the climate in Washington these days, is that people want to have debates on television and elsewhere, but then you want to throw the people who start the debates in jail.”</p>
<p>It was a sharp observation. Jeffery Toobin didn&#8217;t have much of a reply.</p>
<p>The exchange begins at 7:30 on the clip below:</p>
<p><iframe src="//www.youtube.com/embed/d53QM8tnQUQ" height="315" width="420" allowfullscreen="" frameborder="0"></iframe></p>
<p>Ever since The Guardian <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/world/2013/jun/06/nsa-phone-records-verizon-court-order">began</a> to publish its revelations from the files of Edward Snowden, I have been trying to frame the unanswered question that drives my own interest in the subject.</p>
<p>Disclosure: I am not pro-Snowden or anti-Snowden, because to put it that way unnecessarily personalizes the issue. I am not &#8220;for&#8221; the National Security Agency or against it. As a U.S. citizen I am implicated in what the NSA does, and I want it to succeed in discovering those who would harm us. My concern, as a writer and journalism professor, is with another fight: the one for public knowledge, for sunlight, for the facts to come out so we know what&#8217;s going on. I am primarily interested in the journalism that Edward Snowden has set in motion, and the gains in public knowledge that have resulted from his actions, which I have called <a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/07/the-snowden-effect-definition-and-examples/">the Snowden effect</a>.</p>
<p>The question that bothers me most can be put this way:</p>
<blockquote><p>Can there even be an informed public and consent-of-the-governed for decisions about electronic surveillance, or have we put those principles aside so that the state can have its freedom to maneuver?</p></blockquote>
<p>I call it unanswered but it&#8217;s more than that. It&#8217;s like we can&#8217;t face it, so we choose not to frame it that way. The question is less unaddressed than it is repressed by a political system that can&#8217;t handle the weight of what it&#8217;s done. But now that system is being <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2013/07/29/us/politics/momentum-builds-against-nsa-surveillance.html?pagewanted=all&amp;_r=0">forced to face</a> what happened while it wasn&#8217;t looking— at itself. I will show you the problem by quoting four writers who have touched on it. Apologies for the long quotes. But if you can follow me through them, there will be a payoff (in pressthink) at the end.</p>
<p><strong>1. Everything from everybody</strong></p>
<p>Fred Kaplan is on the war and peace beat for Slate. (He also has a PhD in political science.) <a href="http://www.slate.com/articles/news_and_politics/war_stories/2013/08/national_security_agency_s_surveillance_scandal_how_the_nsa_can_regain_americans.single.html">Here</a> he explains &#8220;how the NSA traveled down a slippery slope.&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;At first, they focused on tracking traffic patterns. Some phone number in the United States was calling suspicious people or places in, say, Pakistan. It might be useful to find out whose phone number it was. It might then be useful to find out what other people that person has been calling or emailing, and then it might be useful to track <em>their</em> phone calls and email patterns. Before you know it, they’re storing data on millions of people, including a lot of Americans. Then maybe one day, they track someone—a phone number or email address they’d never come across before—engaged in some very suspicious activity. They wish that they’d been tracking this person for some time, so they could go back and see if a pattern exists without having to wait for one to emerge. Then they learn that they <em>can</em> do this; new technology makes it possible. So they scoop up and store <em>everything</em> from <em>everybody</em>. They even convince themselves that they’re not &#8216;collecting&#8217; data from American citizens (as that would be illegal); no, they’re just <em>storing</em> it; the <em>collecting</em> doesn’t happen until they actually go retrieve it from the files.&#8221;</p>
<p>Somewhere on the slope it slid down, the NSA realizes it has to get legal authority to <a href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/world/national-security/for-nsa-chief-terrorist-threat-drives-passion-to-collect-it-all/2013/07/14/3d26ef80-ea49-11e2-a301-ea5a8116d211_print.html">collect it all</a>. Kaplan explains:</p>
<div>
<blockquote><p>So they ask the FISA court—created by Congress in the 1978 Foreign Intelligence Surveillance Act—to rule on whether this is permissible, and the court complies. Specifically, it rules that they can do this, as long as the material they’re storing is “relevant” to an investigation of terrorism, and the court buys the logic that the agency might need to go fetch data retroactively in such a probe. Therefore, <em>everything </em>is “relevant.”</p></blockquote>
</div>
<blockquote>
<div>
<p>The catch, as we now know, is that all of this—the ever-expanding surveillance in time and space, the reasoning behind it, and the FISA court ruling that approves it—has evolved at such high levels of secrecy that only a handful of people in Congress (very few people anywhere outside the NSA, and probably not all that many inside) know anything about it. This, it turns out, is what [Ron] Wyden, a member of the Senate Intelligence Committee, meant when he cryptically said, way back in October 2011, that “<a href="http://www.businessinsider.com/senator-there-are-two-patriot-acts-and-you-dont-know-about-one-of-them-2011-10" target="_blank">there are two Patriot Acts in America</a>”—the one that anybody can read and a “secret interpretation that the executive branch uses” but that nobody on the outside knows about at all. The public Patriot Act allows “bulk” collection of data; the secret interpretation defines “bulk” far more bulkily than anyone could have imagined.</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<div>
<blockquote><p>And here’s the problem. The program was supposed to have checks and balances, but really it doesn’t, not anymore.</p></blockquote>
</div>
<p>&#8220;They scoop up and store <em>everything</em> from <em>everybody</em>.&#8221; The American public didn&#8217;t know about that. The American people never voted for that. It&#8217;s <a href="http://www.people-press.org/2013/07/26/government-surveillance-a-question-wording-experiment/">doubtful</a> that, sufficiently informed, public opinion would consent to that. Even so, it&#8217;s possible to argue that &#8220;consent of the governed &#8221; was maintained because key committees in Congress were informed. Congress also created the FISA court and gave it these powers.</p>
<p>But that&#8217;s not enough. &#8220;Blind&#8221; consent <a href="http://www.schneier.com/blog/archives/2013/08/restoring_trust.html">can easily be abused</a>. The only possible check on that is public knowledge and open debate. Not for everything, of course (there have to be secrets) but for really big things like &#8220;scoop up and store everything from everybody.&#8221; Not consent at some point in the process but <em>informed consent</em> for big decisions throughout the process: that is the check. It&#8217;s impossible when everything is done in secret, which is why Kaplan says: &#8220;The program was supposed to have checks and balances, but really it doesn’t.&#8221;</p>
<p><strong>2.  A discussion the public cannot afford to have. </strong></p>
<p>Will Wilkinson <a href="http://www.economist.com/blogs/democracyinamerica/2013/07/secret-government">in The Economist</a> picks up the story from there. He&#8217;s more alarmed than Kaplan.</p>
<blockquote><p>You see, democracy here at home must be balanced against the requirements of security, and it is simply too dangerous to leave the question of this balance to the democratic public. Open deliberation over the appropriate balance would require saying something concrete about threats to public safety, and also about the means by which those threats might be checked. But revealing such information would only empower America&#8217;s enemies and endanger American lives. Therefore, this is a discussion Americans can&#8217;t afford to have. Therefore, the power to determine that this is a discussion the public cannot afford to have cannot reside in the democratic public. That power must reside elsewhere, with the best and brightest, with those who have surveyed the perils of the world and know what it takes to meet them. Those deep within the security apparatus, within the charmed circle, must therefore make the decision, on America&#8217;s behalf, about how much democracy—about how much discussion about the limits of democracy, even—it is safe for Americans to have.</p></blockquote>
<p>A &#8220;discussion the public cannot afford to have&#8221; repeals the principle of an informed citizenry. But the decision to let things evolve in that way was hidden from public view. Those who wanted to show it to us were prevented from doing so&#8211; or prosecuted in the case of some whistle blowers. Wilkinson:</p>
<blockquote><p>It is crucial, then, that any attempt by those on the inside to reveal the real, secret rules governing American life be met with overwhelming, intimidating retaliation. In order to maintain a legitimizing democratic imprimatur, it is of course important that a handful of elected officials be brought into the anteroom of the inner council, but it&#8217;s important that they know barely more than that there is a significant risk that we will all perish if they, or the rest of us, know <em>too</em> much, and they must be made to feel that they dare not publicly speak what little they have been allowed to know. Even senators. Even senators must fear to describe America&#8217;s laws to America&#8217;s citizens.</p></blockquote>
<p>An informed public is not really possible under those conditions. And yet those conditions were approved by elected representatives. But they never shared with the voters what they were doing. Some in Congress <a href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/the-white-house-should-end-the-bulk-collection-of-americans-phone-records/2013/07/26/c3c0103e-f553-11e2-9434-60440856fadf_print.html">realized the problem</a> but they were helpless to do anything about it.</p>
<p><strong>3. Free to mislead us.</strong></p>
<p>Here&#8217;s Timothy Edgar, a lawyer who was on the inside (he worked for the Director of National Intelligence) <a href="http://online.wsj.com/article/SB10001424127887323309404578617463152440542.html">writing</a> in the Wall Street Journal.</p>
<blockquote><p>The FISA court may have reviewed the programs, but the public never got its day in court. The ACLU has challenged the constitutionality of NSA surveillance programs for years, but that case never got to the issue of constitutional rights. The intelligence community argued, and the Supreme Court agreed, that the civil-liberties groups couldn&#8217;t maintain their lawsuit. Civil-liberties advocates represented a variety of people with entirely reasonable fears of monitoring. Whether they were actually under surveillance was a secret (and properly so). The government argued vigorously that this secrecy meant the case could not go forward, and the court agreed.</p></blockquote>
<p>Aye: &#8220;The public never got its day in court.&#8221; As with the legal system, so with the Congress. Open debate never got its chance. Edgar :</p>
<blockquote><p>Sens. Ron Wyden and Mark Udall encountered a similar Catch-22 in 2011 when trying to raise questions about the NSA call-records program, when the Patriot Act was up for review. Although they were briefed on the program behind closed doors, they made no headway in arguing for greater transparency with the public. The resulting debate was highly skewed. Administration officials were free to make misleading arguments that the Patriot Act was just like an ordinary subpoena. Any member of Congress willing to spend a few hours in a small room in the Capitol knew that secret court opinions had approved collection that reached far wider than any subpoena. Those who did know about the opinions could not express any concerns in open debate. Secrecy prevented the Congress, like the Supreme Court, from having a real argument over surveillance powers.</p></blockquote>
<p>No real argument = zero possibility for informed consent. Administration officials who are free to mislead are liberated from the check that a free press and an inquisitive Congress are supposed to provide. A democracy that is not permitted to know the &#8220;secret rules&#8221; adopted by the state cannot operate properly. Will Wilkinson thinks the whole system is illegitimate, a sham. Timothy Edgar thinks the public would approve if it knew more about the safeguards in place. Fred Kaplan is somewhere in the middle; he thinks the NSA is in trouble, and recommends reforms. But they all agree that the necessary checks and balances had disappeared.</p>
<p>These include an informed public. That principle had been repealed for scrutiny of the surveillance state. But no one ever put it that way. And they still don&#8217;t. It was too big to face, so bit-by-bit a decision was made not to face it. <em>Repressed</em> is the right word for that.</p>
<p><strong>4. A choice not to be informed. </strong></p>
<p>Edward Snowden is the return of the repressed. By going AWOL and leaking documents that show what the NSA is up to, he forced Congress to ask itself: did we really consent to that? With his disclosures the principle of an informed public roared back to life. <a href="http://tv.msnbc.com/2013/07/31/congress-told-of-extent-of-nsa-surveillance-program-years-ago/">Writing at MSNBC.com</a> about documents de-classified by the Director of National Intelligence in advance of his testimony before the Senate, Adam Serwer puts it all together:</p>
<blockquote><p>The reports affirm that the current backlash in Congress is a product of public knowledge of the programs. Some legislators, like Democratic Sens. Ron Wyden of Oregon and Mark Udall of Colorado, had been making public statements for years that hinted at information members of Congress were being told in private. Legislators who say they were ignorant about how the authorities were being used prior to the revelations effectively made a choice not to be informed. They then voted to reauthorize these laws without knowing what they actually did. Those legislators who were exercising their oversight responsibilities and were concerned about surveillance couldn’t inform the public in detail about what was happening. Far from affirming the Obama administration’s insistence that congressional oversight serves as a key check on executive branch authority, it mostly raises the question of whether effective oversight can be conducted in secret.</p></blockquote>
<p>Some in Congress &#8220;made a choice not to be informed.&#8221; Those who tried to exercise oversight were prevented from informing the rest of us. (But Bruce Ackerman <a href="http://www.foreignpolicy.com/articles/2013/08/1/breach_or_debate_congress_snowden_prism%20?page=full">says</a> they could have busted through that.) Then Snowden came along and blew everything up. Adam Serwer comments:</p>
<blockquote><p>Absent former NSA contractor Edward Snowden’s leaks to <em>The Guardian</em> and the <em>Washington Post </em>that sparked the controversy, the current debate over surveillance powers wouldn’t even be happening. As long as the information was secret, legislators could renew these authorities without having to worry about a public backlash. It was only the leaks that spurred Congress into doing its job. That leaves legislators pushing to curtail government surveillance powers in the awkward position of owing their political momentum to a man the Obama administration wants extradited and prosecuted for espionage.</p></blockquote>
<p>Right. It&#8217;s the same contradiction Jeffrey Toobin of CNN is caught in. He doesn&#8217;t think Snowden should have done what he did. But Toobin <em>does</em> think the debate that Snowden started &#8220;is a good thing.&#8221; When James Risen pointed this out (&#8220;We wouldn&#8217;t be having this discussion if it wasn&#8217;t for him&#8221;) Toobin — mindlessly — agreed. &#8220;That&#8217;s true,&#8221; he said. (It&#8217;s at 8:15 on the clip.) At this point Toobin has no idea what he&#8217;s saying. The only coherent position available to him is to argue <em>for</em> the repeal of an informed public: &#8220;Piers, this debate we&#8217;re having right now, it&#8217;s a bad thing!&#8221;</p>
<p>But he doesn&#8217;t have the courage or self-awareness required to say that on television. At some point he internalized the idea of a &#8220;discussion the public cannot afford to have,&#8221; then repressed any memory of having done that. That&#8217;s why he welcomes a debate that he would also welcome the prevention of. But Congress did the same thing. <a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/07/the-snowden-effect-definition-and-examples/#p19">So did</a> President Obama. The Toobin principle: repeal the concept of an informed public, repress your decision to take such a drastic step because it&#8217;s too much to face.</p>
<p>Let me go back to my unanswered question:</p>
<blockquote><p>Can there even be an informed public and consent-of-the-governed for decisions about electronic surveillance, or have we put those principles aside so that the state can have its freedom to maneuver?</p></blockquote>
<p>People who make a career in journalism cannot pretend to neutrality on a matter like that. If a free society needs them — and I think it does — it needs them to stand strongly against the eclipse of informed consent. <img alt="" src="http://laurenmichell.com/pressthink/wp-content/uploads/2010/10/glasses.png" /></p>
<h5>After Matter: Notes, Reactions &amp; Links</h5>
<p>Over at the Atlantic site, Conor Friedersdorf <a href="http://www.theatlantic.com/politics/print/2013/08/how-secrecy-has-already-corroded-our-democracy-in-concrete-ways/278478/">builds upon this post</a> by going back to look at how the debate over, and news coverage of the re-authorization of the Patriot Act in 2011 was disabled by secrecy. This made informed decision-making and genuine public opinion impossible.</p>
<blockquote><p>Even those elected representatives informed about the full extent of government surveillance were deprived of normal legislative practices, like floor debate, letters and phone calls from constituents, input from experts outside government, and public opinion polls, that properly factor into their typical deliberation and voting decisions.</p></blockquote>
<p>That&#8217;s it, exactly, Conor.</p>
<p>Security and privacy expert Bruce Schneier <a href="http://www.schneier.com/blog/archives/2013/08/restoring_trust.html">writes on the virtually the same subject I addressed here</a>:</p>
<blockquote><p>Both government agencies and corporations have cloaked themselves in so much secrecy that it&#8217;s impossible to verify anything they say; revelation after revelation demonstrates that they&#8217;ve been lying to us regularly and tell the truth only when there&#8217;s no alternative.</p>
<p>There&#8217;s much more to come. Right now, the press has published only a tiny percentage of the documents Snowden took with him. And Snowden&#8217;s files are only a tiny percentage of the number of secrets our government is keeping, awaiting the next whistle-blower.</p>
<p>Ronald Reagan once said &#8220;trust but verify.&#8221; That works only if we can verify. In a world where everyone lies to us all the time, we have no choice but to trust blindly, and we have no reason to believe that anyone is worthy of blind trust. It&#8217;s no wonder that most people are ignoring the story; it&#8217;s just too much cognitive dissonance to try to cope with it.</p></blockquote>
<p>That&#8217;s why I used the word &#8220;repressed.&#8221;</p>
<p>Ars Technica <a href="http://arstechnica.com/tech-policy/2013/08/if-bruce-schneier-ran-the-nsa-hed-ask-a-basic-question-does-it-do-any-good/">asked Schneier</a> (&#8220;one of the world&#8217;s foremost cryptographers&#8221;) what he would do to fix the problems with trust and verification:</p>
<blockquote><p>The way Schneier sees it, in an attempt to keep the operational details of the targets secret, the NSA (and presumably other intelligence agencies, too) has also claimed that it also needs to keep secret the legal justification for what it’s doing. “That’s bullshit,” Schneier says.</p>
<p>The famed computer scientist wants to apply traditionally open and public scrutiny to how the NSA operates.</p>
<p>“How much does this stuff cost and does it do any good?” he said. “And if they can’t tell us that, they don’t get approved. Let’s say the NSA costs $100 million annually and that an FBI agent is $100,000 a year. Is this worth 1,000 FBI agents? Or half and half? Nowhere will you find that analysis.”</p></blockquote>
<p>When the CEO and editor-at-large of Foreign Policy magazine is <a href="http://www.foreignpolicy.com/articles/2013/08/01/declaring_an_end_to_the_decade_of_fear?page=0,0&amp;page=full">saying</a>:<em> it took me a while, but now I see why Snowden was necessary&#8230; </em>it means the elites in Washington are waking up to something big.</p>
<blockquote><p>Eugene Robinson of the <i>Washington Post</i> <a href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/eugene-robinson-snowden-performed-a-valuable-service/2013/07/29/b232965a-f866-11e2-8e84-c56731a202fb_print.html" target="_blank">did</a> D.C. residents a big favor earlier this week when he courageously acknowledged the service Edward Snowden did for the United States&#8230; and for the global debate on rights and privacy in the big data era. I have myself been too slow to recognize that the benefits we have derived from Snowden&#8217;s revelations substantially outweigh the costs associated with the breach. It is time we move from the kind of Patriot Act thinking that overstates security threats to such a degree that we subordinate our basic freedoms to something more consistent with our historical systems of checks and balances.</p></blockquote>
<p>Law professor and former Assistant Attorney General Jack Goldsmith <a href="http://www.lawfareblog.com/2013/08/reflections-on-nsa-oversight-and-a-prediction-that-nsa-authorities-and-oversight-and-transparency-will-expand/">on a theme</a> similar to the one I explore here:</p>
<blockquote><p>Presumptively in our democracy, important national policies are vetted in public, subject to criticism and analysis in the press and by elected representatives and civil society and courts, and ultimately approved, or not, by the People in elections. The accountability system forces public officials to justify their actions, to address criticisms, to confront new and critical information and arguments, to consider new approaches, and to correct mistakes. This messy process does not always produce optimal policies. But it produces pretty good policies on the whole, allows for pretty robust policy change in light of new information, and in any event is a more legitimate system for executing public policy than one that takes place in secret.</p>
<p>Few of the traditional elements of democratic scrutiny and deliberation apply to the NSA. Even after the Snowden affair, NSA and its oversight bodies remain extraordinarily secretive.</p></blockquote>
<p>In a <a href="https://www.techdirt.com/articles/20130810/09240524136/jennifer-hoelzers-insiders-view-administrations-response-to-nsa-surveillance-leaks.shtml">guest post at TechDirt</a>, Jennifer Hoelzer, a former staffer for Senator Ron Wyden, provides enormous detail on how for years he was prevented from informing the public about where the surveillance state had gotten to in the U.S. It is quite a tale.</p>
<p><a href="http://reason.com/blog/2013/07/24/white-house-totally-ready-to-have-that-c">Matt Welch of Reason magazine</a> on the Obama Administration observance of the Toobin principle:</p>
<blockquote><p>We are totally ready to have this conversation we have been actively trying to suppress, we are being transparent in our non-transparency, and in the name of openness and deliberation we must smother this attempt at open deliberation. </p></blockquote>
<p>At ThinkProgress, Zack Beauchamp responds to this post, usefully: <a href="http://thinkprogress.org/security/2013/08/06/2423191/surveillance-democracy-nsa/">A Guide To Thinking About NSA Surveillance And Democracy</a>.</p>
<p>Josh Stearns <a href="http://stearns.wordpress.com/2013/08/09/informed-public/">adds further background</a> and fleshes out the argument in this post. Stearns reminded me of this: “My sole motive,” Edward Snowden told the Guardian in his <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/world/video/2013/jun/09/nsa-whistleblower-edward-snowden-interview-video">first public interview</a>, “is to inform the public as to that which is done in their name and that which is done against them.”</p>
<p>&#8220;A grandiose narcissist who deserves to be in prison.&#8221; <a href="http://www.newyorker.com/online/blogs/comment/2013/06/edward-snowden-nsa-leaker-is-no-hero.html?currentPage=all">Jeffery Toobin on Snowden in June</a>:</p>
<blockquote><p>The American government, and its democracy, are flawed institutions. But our system offers legal options to disgruntled government employees and contractors. They can take advantage of federal whistle-blower laws; they can bring their complaints to Congress; they can try to protest within the institutions where they work. But Snowden did none of this. Instead, in an act that speaks more to his ego than his conscience, he threw the secrets he knew up in the air—and trusted, somehow, that good would come of it. We all now have to hope that he’s right.</p></blockquote>

		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-jay-rosen bypostauthor odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 parent" id="comment-66404">
				<div id="div-comment-66404" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn"><a href='http://journalism.nyu.edu' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>Jay Rosen</a></cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66404">
			August 6, 2013 at 3:46 pm</a>		</div>

		<p>Andrew Tyndall of the <a href="http://tyndallreport.com/" rel="nofollow">The Tyndall Report</a> emails:</p>
<blockquote><p>The <i>Ecuadorian Library</i>, the <a href=“https://medium.com/geek-empire-1/a1ebd2b4a0e5” target=“_blank” rel="nofollow">Bruce Sterling article</a> you linked to from your Twitter feed, makes the observation that we cannot separate the phenomenon of the national security state from other, commercial, manifestations of Big Data: the Googles, the Facebooks, the Amazons, the Internet itself.</p>
<p>For sure, the powers of the state and the powers of the corporation are not one and the same. Yet I find it persuasive to spend more time considering their similarities than their differences.</p>
<p>The phenomenon of Big Data is unreservedly a <i>good thing</i>: it enables us to live our lives in a more targeted, more autonomous, more differentiated way. It improves the quality of the journalism we engage with, the goods we consume, the culture that enriches us.</p>
<p>You are right to argue that our informed consent is indispensible to our enjoyment of the benefits of Big Data. In other words, Big Data should be under our control since without us, as a society, it could not exist in the first place. It is nothing more than the sum total of our collective activities.</p>
<p>There is a second requirement too: that the operation of Big Data be transparent, since opacity prevents control.</p>
<p>So it is at the intersection of these two principles &#8212; control and transparency &#8212; that the questions for the national security state reside. The more it wants to keep secrets, the less massive its data collection must be. The more it wants Big Data, the less secretive it must be.</p>
<p>There is nothing inherently wrong with either. The current crisis arises from the national security state’s desire to have both.</p></blockquote>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>
				</div>

				<div id="div-comment-66407" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">Steiner</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66407">
			August 6, 2013 at 5:28 pm</a>		</div>

		<p>&#8220;Can there even be an informed public and consent-of-the-governed for decisions about electronic surveillance&#8221;<br />
-<br />
Yes, certainly. It&#8217;s not difficult at all.</p>
<p>The public merely needs the truth about the overall surveillance in general Who/What/Why terms;  no need for the highly technical (&#8216;secret&#8217;) details on exactly How it&#8217;s done.</p>
<p>It&#8217;s hardly a public newsflash that the NSA has been collecting foreign government electronic/communications data around the world in large amounts, for half a century&#8230; with sophisticated, ever improving technology.  We knew who the NSA was, what they were doing {generally}, and why they were doing it.</p>
<p>And certainly foreign governments &amp; potential U.S. enemies were also well aware of what the NSA was all about.</p>
<p>What is &#8220;new&#8221; publicly (thanks to Snowden) is that NSA now targets Everyone/Everywhere (including Americans) via a surprisingly vast, covert telecommunications/internet electronic collection system&#8211; feeding a massive database/analysis system.</p>
<p>There&#8217;s no reason this new NSA activity could not have been made public and debated in Congress/media &#8212; except that it&#8217;s blatantly non-Constitutional, illegal, and represents massive criminality. Hence the strong efforts by NSA and many other Federal agencies/persons &#8212; to keep it totally secret from the public.</p>
<p>The NSA&#8217;s long criminal history was first publicly uncovered by the U.S. Senate Church Committee in the 1970&#8242;s. No one was punished; instead Congress invented the unconstitutional &#8220;FISA&#8221; secret court to rubber-stamp future NSA wrongdoing. It should be obvious to all that &#8220;secret courts&#8221; can not exist in a free society.</p>
<p>There&#8217;s nothing unique about an &#8220;informed public and consent-of-the-governed&#8221; regarding electronic surveillance &#8212; it&#8217;s as easy as many other similar issues in U.S. Defense policy and Justice operations.</p>
<p>But Power Corrupts;  there&#8217;s no reason whatsoever for the citizenry to trust the NSA at all.</p>

		<li class="comment even depth-2" id="comment-66630">
				<div id="div-comment-66630" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">joe</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66630">
			August 8, 2013 at 6:23 am</a>		</div>

		<p>1, 2, 3 are consistent in a legalistic frame, in that they are consistent with a few possible worlds.</p>
<p>In a scientific frame, they are inconsistent in that you will in the real world fail to see that sequence with any regularity. We&#8217;re in the process of seeing right now whether it will even happen sufficiently on the multidecade timescale.</p>
<p>We&#8217;ve had this process ongoing for at least a decade (who knows how much longer). We&#8217;ve only seen one effective whistleblower, and a few others who&#8217;ve been ignored and had their careers destroyed, because in practice, those three beliefs are in most cases inconsistent in conjunction with the known pattern of human behavior.</p>
<p>We&#8217;re not in a court of law. We&#8217;re not in a platonic mathematical universal, where the only questions are necessary, sufficient or inconsistent.</p>
<p>We&#8217;re in a world of substance where the question of the probability of a stable social reality is the most important question, whether something happens in fact and how often.</p>
<p>To make it simple, it is physically possible for objects to tunnel through other objects. It is &#8220;consistent&#8221; with reality that a car sits in a garage with all doors closed, and a second later it sits outside the garage, without any door opening in the intervening period.</p>
<p>However, anyone who would make that claim of consistency, if they weren&#8217;t just making a thought experiment in quantum physics, would be either insane or a liar, because in fact the probability is infinitesimal.</p>
<p>You can argue coherently that democracy is only practical for a subset of problems. But you can&#8217;t argue coherently that your &#8220;three possibilities&#8221; are sociologically consistent.</p>
<p>It seems like the pushback to this is almost theological, rather than an actual discussion. It&#8217;s meta-meta-meta, about what we can and can&#8217;t talk about, not about the real facts of the matter. It&#8217;s like a waking dream.</p>
<p>And that dreaming position is taken by those who ultimately claim to be pragmatic and non-ideological. Amazing.</p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>
				</div>

		<p>&#8220;Disclosure: I am not pro-Snowden or anti-Snowden, because to put it that way unnecessarily personalizes the issue. I am not “for” the National Security Agency or against it. As a U.S. citizen I am implicated in what the NSA does, and I want it to succeed in discovering those who would harm us. My concern, as a writer and journalism professor, is with another fight: the one for public knowledge, for sunlight, for the facts to come out so we know what’s going on.&#8221;</p>
<p>This is disingenuous, not to mention professorial.  The debate about the role of the secret police in a democratic society is not like other policy questions.  If you are in favour of sunlight and an informed public, you HAVE to be against a central role for the secret police in the control of knowledge.</p>

		<p>Just so. While Glenn Greenwald can be effusive in his praise of Mr. Rosen, I find the professor&#8217;s continual qualification and equivocating tends to undermine his arguments. Two years ago Mr. Rosen heaped praise on the dreadful NPR simply because they announced that they would discontinue the useless practice of &#8220;he said, she said&#8221; journalism.  He hasn&#8217;t followed up to discuss whether or not NPR honored the pledge.  Perhaps he has made the choice not to be informed.  But more likely he refuses to choose at all.  Part of Mr. Rosen&#8217;s appeal as a critic or analyst of &#8220;the media&#8221; is his evident commitment to avoid acknowledgement of all but the most flagrantly apparent controversies.  He seemed to probe and write with some candor when he famously identified the &#8220;church of the savvy.&#8221;  But as I look back, he did not so much expose a shameful secret as label a trend.  Labeling trends is the meat of modern journalism, like listicles.  His recent flogging of &#8220;The Snowden Effect&#8221; is rather weak tea.  We can&#8217;t avoid the issue, and what else might one call it?  Not whistle-blowing.  Not treason.  Mr. Rosen would never stick his neck out and make that kind of commitment.  He would lose favor with half of his readers if he picked a label like that.  Better to use &#8220;Snowden Effect,&#8221; and provide as little meaning as possible.</p>
<p>So we end up with his tweaking of Jeffrey Toobin.  Mr. Rosen can&#8217;t let him have it.  Even in the pursuit of &#8220;sunlight.&#8221;  Sunlight is no match for &#8220;he said she said.&#8221;</p>

		<p>The premise for this article seems a little flawed.  It presumes that because the ensuing debate has been positive, Snowden&#8217;s actions are necessarily legal or justifiable. That isn&#8217;t so. We have rules all over the place in courts of law regarding the admissibility of evidence, even if catching bad guys is ultimately a positive thing.  The case to justify Snowden&#8217;s abuse of his own power to disclose these documents to which he was privy is ironic considering the subject matter.</p>

				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn"><a href='http://journalism.nyu.edu' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>Jay Rosen</a></cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>

		<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment odd alt depth-3" id="comment-66576">
				<div id="div-comment-66576" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn"><a href='http://www.themisfortuneteller.com' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>Michael Murry</a></cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66576">
			August 8, 2013 at 12:09 am</a>		</div>

		<p>A good and timely article, Mr. Rosen. I especially noted with interest the first part of your title: &#8220;Repeal the concept of an informed public, repress your decision to take such a drastic step.&#8221; It put me immediately in mind of George Orwell&#8217;s definition of <i>doublethink</i>, or double-bind doctrinaire schizophrenia: namely, &#8220;the power of holding two contradictory beliefs in one&#8217;s mind simultaneously, and accepting both of them.&#8221; Jeffrey Toobin&#8217;s performance in this instance surely rates some sort of Orwellian award for <i>doublethink</i> personified.</p>
<p>As Orwell noted, a Corporate Party functionary like Jeffrey Toobin must psychologically repress the obvious import of what he proposes &#8212; criminalizing investigative journalism &#8212; in order to come across as sincere in his outrageous proposal without suffering the cognitive dissonance that would normally accompany such an equally obvious betrayal of journalistic truth. As Orwell put it:</p>
<p>&#8220;The process has to be conscious, or it would not be carried out with sufficient precision, but it also has to be unconscious, or it would bring with it a feeling of falsity and hence of guilt.&#8221;</p>
<p>Mr Toobin cannot with a straight face just come out and shout the Party slogan &#8220;Ignorance is Strength,&#8221; even though he obviously believes that the Corporate Party with two right-wings (thank you, Gore Vidal) must keep Americans ignorant and under constant surveillance in the interests of the Party&#8217;s perpetuation of itself in power. So he must, on the one hand, pay lip service &#8212; on a general level &#8212; to the need of a democratic society for truthful information.</p>
<p>Yet on the other hand, he must denigrate and vilify and condemn every practical avenue that the American people have for assuring themselves of this necessary information. This he does by taking refuge in the lazy semantic abstraction, &#8220;breaking the law,&#8221; which sounds defensible unless one distinguishes between <b>misdemeanor whistleblowing</b> versus <b>felony murders</b>. Both constitute &#8220;breaking the law,&#8221; but common sense and context can easily distinguish the vast difference between them. Jeffrey Toobin obviously cannot (or chooses not to)  make the necessary distinctions, so he confuses and conflates wildly different levels of abstraction, ultimately looking the complete fool.</p>
<p>Pathetic, really. But it seems to pay well. So, good work if you can get it.</p>

		<div class="reply">

		<li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 parent" id="comment-66488">

						<cite class="fn">gorilla cookies</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66488">
			August 7, 2013 at 10:55 am</a>		</div>

		<p>First off, I agree it&#8217;s disingenuous for the Prof to say he&#8217;s not for Snowden, when he obviously is.</p>
<p>Second, there isn&#8217;t really much of a contradiction in what Toobin said, even if Rosen fails to see it. It is possible to think that it&#8217;s good to have more openness about our national security apparatus, without approving of what Snowden did. There have been many articles over the years on what the NSA has been doing. Not all of them were sensationalized like the Guardian&#8217;s coverage was, which is why the Snowden revelations have received more attention. But those articles do prove it is possible to have a debate on this topic without someone having to commit a felony.</p>
<p>Moreover, we can&#8217;t just talk about Snowden as a &#8220;whistleblower&#8221; in the abstract. He is a real human being who committed a series of specific actions.</p>
<p>If we trust Glenn Greenwald &#8211; and I know, that&#8217;s not the most reliable source &#8211; Snowden stole documents that could endanger the security of our country which he is keeping for &#8220;insurance.&#8221; He stole documents and gave them to Greenwald that were so sensitive even Greenwald (still) doesn&#8217;t want to print him. We still don&#8217;t know how much he has told the Russians and the Chinese. We still don&#8217;t know whether some foreign governments have gotten ahold of the documents he stole. (If you think these governments are helping him out out of the goodness of their heart, you are crazy.) We still don&#8217;t know how much he has damaged the security of the U.S. These are all open questions, and until they are answered, it&#8217;s silly to say, &#8220;Snowden&#8217;s awesome, because he sparked a debate.&#8221;</p>
<p>If Snowden had just stolen one document &#8211; the Verizon court order on phone metadata &#8211; we would still have had the debate we are having, and he wouldn&#8217;t be in as much trouble he is currently in. Then he could make a fair case that all he was doing was whistle-blowing. The fact that he went farther than that, and stole dangerous documents simply for &#8220;insurance,&#8221; and that it&#8217;s possible those documents have fallen into the hands of some of the most amoral actors on the world stage, is what troubles so many people about his case and his behavior.</p>

						<cite class="fn">gorilla cookies</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66491">
			August 7, 2013 at 11:20 am</a>		</div>

		<p>&gt;&gt;Maybe so. But it is not tenable, if you’re a journalist, to think it’s great to have the debate that Snowden’s actions are directly responsible for, and think that Snowden should not have taken those actions. This is the mindless position Jeffry Toobin worked himself into.&gt;&gt;</p>
<p>Maybe so? Definitely so! You call people who disagree with you &#8220;mindless,&#8221; but you don&#8217;t seem to have thought this through.</p>
<p>This is a gruesome analogy, but the events in Newtown sparked a debate on gun control. The debate was a good thing to have. The actions that led to them were not.</p>
<p>You are saying that journalists have to approve of what Snowden did because his actions &#8220;stirred a debate.&#8221; Many things stir a debate. The Iraq war stirred a debate. Right-wing ranting on immigration stirs a debate. That doesn&#8217;t mean it&#8217;s good. </p>
<p>Here are two questions:</p>
<p>- Could this discussion have come about without Snowden committing a felony?</p>
<p>- Is it possible to disagree with Snowden&#8217;s actions because he did things that went above and beyond simply informing the public?</p>
<p>The answer to the first question is obviously yes, since this subject has been in the press before.</p>
<p>The answer to the second question is yes as well. If all you are after is whistle-blowing, you don&#8217;t steal documents that could endanger the security of this country as &#8220;insurance.&#8221; </p>
<p>Again, if Snowden had taken different actions &#8211; just stolen one or two documents, instead of the large number of apparently sensitive things he stole &#8211; he might be in better shape, and we would still be having the same discussions.  </p>
<p>These aren&#8217;t complicated things, they are issues that go to the heart of the charges against Snowden.</p>

		<p>Your bias is obvious so let me start by laying out mine.  I believe that the US government, by which I mean all three branches, the military and the included bureaucracies have, either knowingly or unknowingly, committed treason (by which I mean violated their oath to defend and protect Constitution) in their pursuit of safety and stability. You might try to argue that this so-called treason is necessary and justifiable&#8230;as some have done by simplistically quoting &#8216;the Constitution is not a suicide pact&#8217;.</p>
<p>The reason I say &#8220;simplistically&#8221; is because, in this age when language is used to support an agenda rather than truthfully communicate (and everything depends on discovering what the meaning of the word &#8216;is&#8217; is) we need to more fully examine the purposes for which we require said level of &#8220;safety and stability&#8221;.  Once we dismiss the transparently ridiculous &#8216;we are good and they are evil&#8217; paradigm and examine the details of the &#8216;war on terror&#8217; it becomes obvious that it is nothing more than the unwinnable (albeit long and drawn out) struggle of a dying empire to maintain its hold on power. A power supported (until recently) in large part by the pretense of our desire to spread &#8216;freedom and democracy&#8217;. A pretense which has now been betrayed by the reality of US support for corrupt, illegitimate regimes which historically have done its dirty work for it.</p>
<p>This was Osama bin Laden&#8217;s primary contribution&#8230;that the source of the oppression and injustice, from which billions of people cry out for relief, is not so much the puppet dictators but rather the puppet master&#8230;and by extension the corporate elite it serves.  The reason so many well meaning people have difficulty making a coherent argument against this police state is that they refuse to acknowledge the length and breadth this lie known as the &#8216;war on terror&#8217; represents. History repeatedly shows us that the most powerful tools of government are fear and hatred.  These tools are most commonly exploited by our innate fear of the &#8216;other&#8217;.  It doesn&#8217;t matter whether it is communism, socialism, fascism, terrorism or some other as yet unnamed &#8216;ism&#8217; it is all just the same recycled propaganda the powerful have used to exploit the powerless since man first organized into groups.  As soon as you establish two or more groups you create &#8216;us&#8217; and &#8216;them&#8217;.  Combine this with ambitious men and you have all the ingredients needed for exploitation to succeed.  It has been framed many ways throughout the ages but it really does come down to the &#8220;red pill/blue pill&#8221; choice.  If you are lucky enough to be born amongst the &#8216;haves&#8217; keep your head down, don&#8217;t ask many questions and life is good.  If you are unlucky enough to be born amongst the &#8216;have nots&#8217; (or with a conscience) however your choices are not so simple.</p>

The reason that Congress is now considering changes to bring more transparency to the process, or even possibly cut off funding for bulk collection, is not because of the sexy &#8220;fugitive angle,&#8221; it is a direct result of what Snowden did.</p>
<p>Final attempt, since you seem determined to misstate my view on this. In my view it remains mindless — that is, without good sense or logic — for Jeffrey Toobin as a journalist to condemn Snowden and reject his actions <i>in the terms he did</i>, and also welcome the debate that sprung from those actions, while at the same time praising Greenwald for investigative reporting. It is possible for a hypothetical observer to object to some of the things Snowden did and also welcome that debate, but I wasn&#8217;t writing about a hypothetical observer when I used the term &#8220;mindlessly&#8221; (adverb, you converted it to an adjective) about a specific moment in a specific exchange on a specific television program.  Good day, sir. </p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>
				</div>
		<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment even depth-5" id="comment-66523">
				<div id="div-comment-66523" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">gorilla cookies</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66523">
			August 7, 2013 at 3:04 pm</a>		</div>

		<p>I agree that we are starting to repeat ourselves, but now you are saying that it isn&#8217;t that just the fact that Toobin disapproves of what Snowden did, you just don&#8217;t like  his terminology. In your post, you note that Toobin called Snowden a criminal. By any standard, he is. What problem do you have with that?</p>
<p>Anyway, you are now saying that you just have an issue with one Snowden critic (Toobin), rather than all of them (though if I&#8217;m not mistaken, you call them &#8220;statists&#8221; in a tweet). But we&#8217;re not talking hypothetical observers here. There are plenty of people who think we need, say, FISA reform but are turned off by Snowden&#8217;s overall action. Just because some of the ends may be good doesn&#8217;t mean we have to justify all of his means.</p>
<p>The larger point, and this was an issue in both the Manning and now Snowden cases, is that we are now in a world where people can steal large amounts of confidential information very easily. Should journalists (and groups like Wikileaks) encourage that? If someone steals a whole bunch of documents, and some of them are valuable information for the public to know, and some of them put people&#8217;s lives at risk, is that a good thing, or a bad thing? And how do journalistic organizations balance the public&#8217;s right to know versus real national security needs?</p>
<p>These aren&#8217;t easy issues, as much as some people would like to paint them as such. And anyone who does is, dare i say it, &#8220;mindless.&#8221;</p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>

The issue here is that, because what has happened lacks the consent of the governed in any sense that is justifiable, whether the United States remains a democracy. On that issue, the burden of proof is on the security state, and as far as I can tell, it has no proof. Consequently, it is slowly dawning on people that the United States is something other than a democracy and the debate is what to call what it has become.</p>

But lets do that without trying to kick someone else out of the club, out of a phantom &#8220;profession.&#8221;</p>
<p>One of the pleasures I found in newspapering was I wasn&#8217;t in a club policed by self-appointed busybodies.</p>

		<li class="comment even thread-even depth-1 parent" id="comment-66489">
				<div id="div-comment-66489" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn"><a href='http://www.hughsansom.com/' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>Hugh Sansom</a></cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66489">
			August 7, 2013 at 11:04 am</a>		</div>

		<p>Several questions or points (apologies for the length):</p>
<p>1. Despite the appearance of contradiction (and while there is a tension), one can be consistent in thinking that the debate resulting from Snowden&#8217;s leaks is good although Snowden&#8217;s actions themselves are bad.</p>
<p>Jeffrey Toobin seems conservative to me. He certainly falls within the spectrum of standard American thinking where actions are justified instrumentally — by virtue of the good outcomes those actions produce. (The most dogmatically held example of this in the US is the conviction that enormous inequality is justified by the &#8216;trickle down&#8217; effect.) So if the debate resulting from Snowden&#8217;s actions is a good thing, Toobin must believe there is some overriding negative outcome that makes Snowden&#8217;s actions bad. This could be a coherent argument, but neither Toobin nor others attacking Snowden make it because there is little real argument nor any wish for such in the mainstream about Snowden.</p>
<p>My suspicion regarding Toobin&#8217;s (and others&#8217;) distress over Snowden&#8217;s leaks is threefold:<br />
   a. Toobin and many journalists, scholars, observers like him (e.g., Matt Yglesias, Chris Hayes, David Gregory, etc.) deeply, personally identify with power, especially Washington (&#8220;This Town&#8221;, as Mark Leibovich has described). They have powerful incentives to do so; their wellbeing as pilot fish depends on that of the sharks.<br />
   b. They therefore see criticism of Obama or the US government as criticism of themselves.<br />
   c. They are profoundly unable to conceive of the possibility that American leaders, in government or business, might be guilty of really awful wrongdoing. This is why years ago, for example, Toobin could casually attack OJ Simpson before the facts were in, but cannot criticize any American leader, like Obama, as a plausible candidate for war crimes charges.</p>
<p>Snowden or Wikileaks generate cognitive dissonance for the Toobins in America. They resolve the dissonance with just-so stories that exonerate American power. If they actually thought about it, they could construct a coherent argument. They are unaccustomed to doing so because the US culture is one that bitterly rejects challenges to power, fashion, wealth, fame.</p>
<p>2. &#8220;[D]emocracy here at home must be balanced against the requirements of security.&#8221; What would be the response to: &#8220;Security here at home must be balanced against the requirements of democracy&#8221;? The notion that democracy brings demands seems to have been lost. </p>
<p>3. How would Obama or Sen. Feinstein or any of those who endlessly defend government abuses react if there were a broad, deep public demand for democracy, defense of rights, and an end to massive surveillance? If we have not already reached the point of no return, we are rapidly approaching one where a surge in public opposition would provoke a constitutional crisis worse than that seen in the Civil War. The crisis will likely never arise because the public is so misinformed, so deceived, and so dogmatic in its faithful attachment to American power that the demand will never be made.</p>
<p>Lest this seem like conspiracy theorizing or just handwaving, recall that in the Nixon years, calls by some within the administration for more troops in Vietnam were opposed because it was thought those troops might be needed <i>in the US</i> to quell domestic unrest. Recall also that both Bush and Obama made legal moves that would, in principle, undermine <i>posse comitatus</i> and allow use of US troops within the US.</p>
<p>4. Despite racist hostility to Obama or malicious GOP opposition to anything Democratic, Americans are still overwhelmingly of the view that we <i>owe obedience</i> to political leaders. Americans identify the powerful in America with America itself. And they suffer under the delusion that they, any day now, will win the lottery and join the powerful.</p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>
				</div>
		<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 parent" id="comment-66522">
				<div id="div-comment-66522" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">Mutex7</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66522">
			August 7, 2013 at 2:54 pm</a>		</div>

		<p>Excellent comments and insight.  This entire debate depends on whether you see the US government as controlled by inherently good and trustworthy people or whether you believe, as the founding fathers did, that all rule by man is inevitably fraught with danger and therefore opted for the rule of law and its incumbent restriction on the power of our representatives.  Lost in all this debate over the expediency of surveillance &#8216;for our protection&#8217; is that our entire legal system is predicated on the presumption of innocence and the belief that it is better to let a hundred criminals potentially go free than to punish men for crimes they didn&#8217;t commit.</p>
<p>It is for this reason all of these activities need to couched and defended under the terms of war&#8230;buy the premise, buy the bit.  This debate is necessarily predicated on the distortion of language.  We have taken a word like &#8216;war&#8217; which had been understood to have a particular meaning for the entire history of mankind and twisted it into being a worldwide conflict against a nebulous enemy, which can&#8217;t surrender and, of which, we continue to create more.  The &#8216;war on terror&#8217; is the perpetual motion machine of conflicts.  Even Rumsfeld once had the insight to ask whether we aren&#8217;t creating more &#8216;terrorists&#8217; than we are killing or capturing. Perhaps this misses the point though. In a military industrial complex, dependent on war for profit, perhaps the whole idea is to never run out of &#8216;terrorists&#8217;.</p>
<p>We live in a nonsensical world where the executive branch needs a warrant to wiretap an American citizen but can kill this very same citizen without approval from any court simply by declaring him a terrorist.  It&#8217;s like &#8216;the emperor&#8217;s new clothes&#8217; meets &#8216;the twilight zone&#8217; as people twist themselves into knots to keep from acknowledging reality.</p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>

		<p>You&#8217;re reading more into it than is there, and ignoring my qualification. The sentence reads, &#8220;I am not pro-Snowden or anti-Snowden, <i>because to put it that way</i> unnecessarily personalizes the issue.&#8221; The point I am trying to make is that I don&#8217;t think Snowden, yay! or Snowden, boo! is a good way of arguing about this case. My views are more complicated than that. And whether he&#8217;s a hero, a narcissist, a creep, etc. clouds the issue. </p>
<p>I think he took a principled action in trying to alert the public about the overreaches of the NSA. I think the debate that&#8217;s followed from his actions has been a good thing, a necessary thing. And I believe those views are made sufficiently clear in this post. I&#8217;m not ready to defend everything he has done or said. I might be critical of part of it, and agree with the rest of it. Thus: not pro, or anti. </p>
<p>I&#8217;m not claiming any sort of &#8220;objectivity,&#8221; a word you imported. I&#8217;m trying to elude crude categories that don&#8217;t help us and don&#8217;t express my thoughts on the matter.</p>

		<p>Excellent piece.</p>
<p>You may want to dig in one step further though:</p>
<p>what is electronic surveillance, in effect, used for &#8211; in the first place ?</p>
<p>(I know: this is not the question you raised.)</p>
<p>But as far as I know and as far as it is documented, electronic surveillance is used primarily for PROTECTION. So what&#8217;s wrong with protection ? Heck who would not protect himself or his/her loved ones ? This is so strong an argument that there is almost no dissent &#8212; the consent is almost obvious and your question</p>
<p>&#8220;Can there even be an informed public and consent-of-the-governed for decisions about electronic surveillance, or have we put those principles aside so that the state can have its freedom to maneuver?&#8221;</p>
<p>is only about the details of that protection that the state/the government/the mafia gang &#8211; oops sorry cross that out- provides you. Should you be privy to those details or not ? It probably depends on your level of closeness to the state/the government, etc.</p>
<p>But &#8212; as you climb up the ladder &#8212; you discover the obvious: the essence of protection and the absolute necessity of electronic surveillance. The state definitely needs to protect itself FROM US. This is what you call &#8220;the state&#8217;s freedom to maneuver&#8221;. And the extreme majority of the use of electronic surveillance is exactly this: to protect the state (and those who run it, I am not talking of you and me) against its own citizens.</p>
<p>So maybe the time is ripe for a real debate on what kind of society we want to live in. Or maybe not. Isn&#8217;t it what your question was actually all about ?</p>
<p>Sorry if I read in too much your piece. <img src='http://pressthink.org/wp-includes/images/smilies/icon_smile.gif' alt=':-)' class='wp-smiley' />  Anyway I liked it.</p>

				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">TuffsNotEnuff</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66541">
			August 7, 2013 at 4:55 pm</a>		</div>

		<p>The first hoax related to NSA&#8217;s PRISM and other spy-on-civilians programs is that NSA management controls what happens with the data they collect. Plainly, NSA and the White House have no idea how the computer systems work &#8212; proven recently when they promised to restrict access to the (irrelevant) query front-end tools.</p>
<p>This is quite a hoax. It also requires you to believe that NSA does not have direct access to emails and other communications. &#8220;Direct,&#8221; maybe not. Indirectly through three-hop data stores at the ISPs &#8212; that&#8217;s what Snowden describes. NSA has to have full linguistic indexes to the ISP archives and that&#8217;s how the techs can get to anything in seconds.</p>
<p>There is a second hoax. That one presents a claim that the Obama White House is accurately informed and controls what happens with these NSA systems. No way.</p>
<p>Obama&#8217;s statements make it clear that he has no idea that NSA has indexed the ISP archives and grabs emails/comm data with no restraints. NSA uses a &#8220;hot pursuit&#8221; equivalent Order from the FISA court. That makes anything and everything arguably legal. Obama has no idea why building linguistic indexes remotely &#8212; at the ISPs, not at NSA &#8212; is critical to implement this three-hop spy system.</p>
<p>As in the dark as Obama is, don&#8217;t imagine that G.W. Bush did any better. NSA made up a story and they have told it to every elected official from 2004 to present. In their own minds, the NSA managers have verbal constructs that avoid lying, while they mislead all of the elected Fools. </p>
<p>Finally, where&#8217;s analysis of the down side risks of having these systems in place ??? What was the FBI Special Agent doing with 9,000,000 citizens&#8217; records on his laptop? Was he out shopping them at $ 0.10 a pop? What happens when the White House gets a president matched to Aaron Burr, J. Edgar Hoover, or Dick Cheney? Corporate media hasn&#8217;t said a word about the down side.</p>

		<p>Gorilla Cookies writes in his last contribution: &#8220;The larger point, and this was an issue in both the Manning and now Snowden cases, is that we are now in a world where people can steal large amounts of confidential information very easily.&#8221;</p>
<p>Yes, that is exactly what the NSA, GCHQ etc. are doing.</p>
<p>Gorilla Cookies continues: &#8220;Should journalists (and groups like Wikileaks) encourage that?&#8221;</p>
<p>The answer is: no, of course they should not encourage such criminal spying on millions of innocent citizens. And luckily, they don´t encourage it, but expose it.</p>
<p>Mr. Gorilla Cookies deplores the fact that Mr. Snowden has broken certain laws, and that some journalists make use of this to make governmental violations of the American constitution known to a large public. He seems to suggest that such journalistic actions, although not illegal, are illegitimate.</p>
<p>I disagree with that. &#8220;Laws&#8221; that prescribe secrecy about governmental criminality, and which have on top of that been put into force without the consent of a majority of well-informed, non-bribed representatives of a well-informed public, are not laws, but a form of omerta &#8211; the obligation of mafia participants to be silent. Even when such &#8220;laws&#8221; are &#8220;legal&#8221; in a formalistic sense, they are not legitimate laws. </p>
<p>In imperial China, the emperor had a &#8220;Mandate of Heaven&#8221;. In absolutist France (before the French Revolution), the Kings regarded themselves as being appointed by God. But even the Mandate of Heaven could be lost. And we all know what happened to the French kings after they had ignored the well-being of their &#8220;99%&#8221; (OK, &#8220;5%&#8221;) for too long. Mr. Gorilla Cookies seems to be interested only in maintaining a facade of paper laws covering up for illegitimate mafia laws, but not in really legitimate laws and their effective implementation.</p>
<p>What I miss in this discussion, is the fact that this is not about a balance between security and democracy, but about a balance between, on the one hand, insecurity as a result of a (partly self-created) risk of terrorism, and, on the other hand, insecurity as a result of a derailed, uncontrolled regime at home. Well-informed democracy is not one side of such a balance, but the best insurance against both kinds of insecurity.</p>
<p>For all I know, Mr. Snowden has acted so far as a true whistleblower: only divulging information that is relevant to the abuses he wanted to draw attention to. The question is not whether Snowden has done too much &#8211; it is quite obvious that without his strategy of enabling journalists to publish pieces of information over a longer stretch of time, his actions would not have been effective, in view of all the lies and other power abuse that are applied in order to suppress his message. The question is whether Snowden has done ENOUGH to create a serious chance to bring back democracy and the rule of law to North America and Europe.</p>
<p>As for newspapers and journalists&#8230; so far, only a few of them are drawing their readers´ attention to what is really at stake here. It is a hopeful miracle that in spite of the massive political efforts to befuddle us, more and more people are becoming conscious of the significance of this issue.</p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>
				</div>

		<p>Could you change &#8216;imprimatur&#8217; to &#8216;nihil obstat&#8217; in the article? Imprimatur and nihil obstat were printed in books authorised by a bishop of the Catholic church &#8211; imprimatur means &#8216;it may be printed&#8217;, and &#8216;nihil obstat&#8217; means &#8216;let no one hinder [the book appearing]&#8216;. In the sentence where imprimatur is used, the author meant just &#8216;permission&#8217;, and here &#8216;consent to keep secrets&#8217; which makes &#8216;imprimatur&#8217; a nonsense, or a very sick piece of irony indeed. Its too subtle a point to be an anticatholic slur, more likely just ignorance of the terms and their usage. Please consider a correction to make it make sense.</p>

		<p>Yes &#8212; finally someone starts to get it.</p>
<p>This mechanism of internal repression &#8212; of questions that can not be asked &#8212; is systematically crucial. Then you can have perfectly open discussions, as long as certain thoughts are off limit by definition. </p>
<p>This is most obvious in the current case, where the issues are off-limit by secrecy, not just the details but the very policy questions themselves. But it&#8217;s not limited to that &#8212; there are a large number of political questions that will immediately brand you as disloyal and anti-American if you even ask them, make the informed consent necessary to claim democratic legitimacy extremely problematic.</p>
<p>And to go further on this story, Reuters is now reporting that the DEA has been using NSA (and other illegal to use in court) tips since the 70s. After making their case, they go back and scrub the history, inventing an initial source distinct from the factual source so that they can make a court case. The IRS apparently has been involved in this as well.</p>
<p>So, we&#8217;re literally rewriting history so that the NSA-type spying can have judicially effectiveness. In fact, repressing our own legal memory.</p>
<p>It&#8217;s truly astounding. It&#8217;s like the crazy-ass postmodernist nutcases were right all along. I&#8217;m expecting evil alien reptilian overlords to unmask themselves any day now.</p>

		<p>RE:</p>
<blockquote>
<p> &#8220;<b>Disclosure: I am not pro-Snowden or anti-Snowden, because to put it that way unnecessarily personalizes the issue. I am not “for” the National Security Agency or against it.</b> As a U.S. citizen I am implicated in what the NSA does, and I want it to succeed in discovering those who would harm us. My concern, as a writer and journalism professor, is with another fight: the one for public knowledge, for sunlight, for the facts to come out so we know what’s going on. I am primarily interested in the journalism that Edward Snowden has set in motion, and the gains in public knowledge that have resulted from his actions, which I have called the Snowden effect. #</p>
<p>The question that bothers me most can be put this way: #</p>
<p>Can there even be an informed public and consent-of-the-governed for decisions about electronic surveillance, or have we put those principles aside so that the state can have its freedom to maneuver?</p>
<p> (snip) </p>
<p> &#8230;&#8221;<b>People who make a career in journalism cannot pretend to neutrality on a matter like that. If a free society needs them — and I think it does — it needs them to stand strongly against the eclipse of informed consent.</b>&#8221;</p>
</blockquote>
<p>  There&#8217;s a great deal I agree with in your article&#8211;&#8221;Glenn Greenwald sent me.&#8221;&#8211; and so, I&#8217;m puzzled over one point and would like to bring it up for more consideration.</p>
<p>  You&#8217;ve aptly noticed some important psychological dysfunctions in the rationalizing of many concerning the whole set of issues in what may be summarized as the Edward Snowden Whistle-blowing affair.  I agree with those points you make in noticing that  J. Toobin and Piers Morgan, cited, and so many others not cited, in effect do this: &#8220;Repeal the concept of an informed public, repress your decision to take such a drastic step.&#8221;</p>
<p>   So I find it interesting that you, yourself, seem to have something of that in your own reasoning: where you find it necessary to place a disclaimer at the start, informing us that you are  &#8220;not pro-Snowden or anti-Snowden.&#8221; </p>
<p>  I don&#8217;t understand that.  Again, the whole point of &#8220;being &#8216;Pro-Snowden&#8217;, after all, concerns specifically his public acts in which he divulged information showing apparent habitual official crimes to the press.  It&#8217;s for this that anyone who is an ordinary observer of public affairs knows about E. Snowden at all.</p>
<p>  Since he didn&#8217;t become notable for some and notorious for others for any other reasons than these disclosures of apparent official criminal behavior, I can&#8217;t see why one ought to, especially given your views, disclaim being &#8220;Pro-Snowden&#8221;.</p>
<p>  I am &#8216;Pro-Snowden&#8217; because I approve of what Mr. Snowden did,  and I’m Pro-Snowden out of respect for his conscientious acts, his moral stand, his courage to see, recognize and act on his moral understanding of the circumstances in which he found himself as an N.S.A. contract employee &#8211;with a working conscience.</p>
<p>  I don&#8217;t understand how, except by doing a bit of psychological compartmentalizing, you are able <b>to maintain both the views, highlighted above in boldface.</b></p>

		<p>&#8220;Being informed is of no value unless one is responsible, which is where Americans seem to come unstuck. Too many avoid responsibility at all costs, wildly abdicating it to anyone who promises to relieve us of it.&#8221;</p>
<p>  That is a very important insight&#8211;and so true.  The current ethos in English-speaking culture, most pronounced in the U.S. version, is precisely this which you describe.  I call it the habit of  &#8220;making a &#8216;virtue&#8217; of any and everything which one believes, rightly or wrongly &#8216;to be unavoidable&#8217;&#8221; and it runs through and through popular opinion on topics of great diversity.  It is essentially a resigned approach to the world, a surrender in advance of a fight, apparently based on the easy refuge of believing that &#8220;there is nothing anyone can do about &#8230;&#8221; (whatever the case may be).  This is reinforced in myriad was, in all sorts of popular mass media&#8211;television, film, recent journalism and books and, still more, all over the discussion fora of the world wide web, which, as a force, do tremendous social harm along with whatever incidental good happens along the way.</p>

		<li class="comment odd alt depth-3 parent" id="comment-66808">
				<div id="div-comment-66808" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">proximity1</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-66808">
			August 9, 2013 at 10:46 am</a>		</div>

		<p>RE: &#8220;I just don’t think “pro-Snowden” is a good way of describing what I am pro. If it works for you in what you support, all good.&#8221;</p>
<p>  I believe that these&#8211;&#8217;Pro-Snowden&#8217; and &#8216;pro-&#8217;his public civic-minded actions&#8217; are, whether recognized or not, and however inconvenient to our post-Englightenment morality, inextricably connected, necessarily connected and I believe that this connection is not only necessary, it&#8217;s for important reasons also &#8216;troublesome&#8217; and that this troublesome aspect drives your taking pains to force a distinction between the person (you won&#8217;t avow an approval for) and what the person did (which acts you do avow approval for).</p>
<p>  This is to take the individual actor and render him or her an abstraction in some supposedly &#8220;larger story&#8221;;  but advocating such a distinction leaves us, I believe, in exactly the same moral peril as those who are still working in jobs such as Mr. Snowden had and who have read the press, thought over the &#8220;issues&#8221; and concluded that they&#8217;re not implicated, since we may divorce a person from his acts, each may find and make his own way as it suits his own &#8220;personal&#8221; proclivities.  That divorces the moral import of acts from their intimately personal connection with those who in fact <i>act</i> and, again, forgive me repeating myself, this leaves us in precisely the sort of situation which is so well and so easily exploited by the very people who have made <i>a target of Snowden personally</i> for what he took upon himself as an act of moral conviction.</p>
<p>  You, I think, ask of us to focus exclusively on the conviction itself as an abstraction, and this lets all of <i>the rest of us</i> who are and who have bene faced with analogous moral dilemmas, from feeling intensely the obligation to respond to the moral demands of our consciences.</p>
<p>   This, I think, is right at the heart of what is most admirable about what <i>Edward Snowden</i> was able to do and what the vast majority of us find beyond our abilities.</p>
<p>  That is a post-Enlightenment view of moral responsibility, in keeping with the way in which our world has taken in so many respects a &#8220;depersonalized&#8221; view of reality.</p>
<p>  But, as any respectable Englightenment thinker would argue, &#8220;No actor?, then no moral act.&#8221;</p>

		<p>Not just &#8220;interesting&#8221;. Essential and vital. Calling it &#8220;interesting&#8221; means that you distance yourself from the moral implication of what Proximity1 says, i.e. its implication for YOU.</p>
<p>Snowden belongs to the small minority of people who want their words and their acts to match each other. That is, the small minority of people who are not cynical or (wilfully) naive. Snowden did not say: &#8220;It is interesting that I am participating in unconstitutional acts that threaten to eliminate democracy and the rule of law.&#8221; He chose not to be cynical, but to stop doing the wrong thing. </p>
<p>In addition, he chose to do something about the wrong thing, at great personal risk. This changed him from a merely non-cynical, decent person into a hero (OK, &#8220;we can´t all be heroes&#8221;&#8230; oops, that is again a cynical thing to say).</p>
<p>A good read on this is Peter Sloterdijk, &#8220;Critique of Cynical Reason&#8221; (Kritik der Zynischen Vernunft). First published in the early eighties of the 20C, it is more relevant than ever.</p>

						<cite class="fn">Michiel Jonker</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
