		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 parent" id="comment-68936">
				<div id="div-comment-68936" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">JOE</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-68936">
			August 17, 2013 at 9:16 am</a>		</div>

		<p>Regarding the banality of evil: I see that discussion often abused, reduced to some kind of banal hypocrisy. Arendt&#8217;s point, in my view, was that a great evil was indeed done, but by a person who justified it in the most banal of ways. Not just following order, but &#8220;simply doing my job&#8221;. Eichmann&#8217;s actual monstrosity was masked by claiming a banal personal virtue, that he paid his rent, was a good neighbor, went to work and just did his job, making no moral judgment on his context.</p>
<p>This is a very chronic disease, the disease of pragmatism as a cover for a great inner immorality, and I believe that it underlies a great deal of this column, to get back to Toobin.</p>
<p>All of this is possible only because people, unlike Snowden, can be banally evil. They take acts as agents, public acts, which are counter to the ideals they claim privately. They&#8217;re &#8220;good liberals&#8221; or some such principled stand, except that in practice and in substance they&#8217;re entire careers undermine that.</p>
<p>Look at Palantir (the company), for example. The top guys are a hippy liberal stoner philosopher, and a libertarian businessman &#8212; and I don&#8217;t doubt that is how they relate personally with friends and family. But in public, they&#8217;re running one of the major spying infrastructures, and use their &#8220;personal ideals&#8221; to escape responsibility from their public actions.</p>
<p>Jay&#8217;s rhetoric isn&#8217;t that kind of thing. He&#8217;s not doing some great evil that requires this many lines of analysis. At best, the appropriate response is &#8220;Sersly? The view from nowhere doesn&#8217;t work here.&#8221;</p>
<p>Important moral choices are being made. This isn&#8217;t one of them.</p>
<p>@Joinkers:<br />
As long as folks see themselves as merely consumers, who&#8217;s labor is simply a means to maximize that consumption, things ain&#8217;t going to get better. If you&#8217;re just a rational market player, then you&#8217;re not actually a moral entity. There&#8217;s your banality of evil, a chain of agents that never end in someone who takes responsibility, a collection of monads simply responding to their environment.</p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>
				</div>
		<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment even depth-3 parent" id="comment-68961">
				<div id="div-comment-68961" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">proximity1</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-68961">
			August 17, 2013 at 1:34 pm</a>		</div>

		<p>I think that the matter of the character of evil as &#8220;banal&#8221; or something else is a very unproductive focus&#8211;but, paradoxically, the reasons why I believe that to be the case <i>are,</i> in my opinion, worth discussion if we found the proper time and venue for it.</p>
<p>  I don&#8217;t think this thread is necessarily that venue&#8211;but I do think that this preoccupation with whether it&#8217;s useful and interesting to debate evil and a banality of it has serious implications for the issues we <i>are</i> concerned with here.</p>
<p>   To do the matter justice, I&#8217;d have to dig out my copy of <i>Eichmann in Jerusalem</i> for a review of the arguments there.  And, while I expect to come across it soon, for other reasons, I won&#8217;t have time in the near term.   All my comments on the issue have been off the cuff, based on my general views of the issues, and without having had recourse to Arendt&#8217;s book.</p>
<p>  Last point&#8211; you&#8217;re assuming, wrongly I think, I (or Michiel) are working from an assumption that Jay is some sort of model of bad moral conduct.  </p>
<p>  So, when you write, </p>
<p>  &#8220;He’s (Jay) not doing some great evil that requires this many lines of analysis,&#8221;</p>
<p>  my reaction is, Indeed, he isn&#8217;t and, by the way, no one here has even implied this, let alone alleged it directly.</p>
<p>  Your comments strike me as coming from a touchy conscience, if not a somehow somewhat guilty one.   I start from the view that Jay and this site are resolutely trying to defend the same causes and principles that I want to defend.  Our discussion is over some arguably important details of how to do that.</p>
<p>  No one that I&#8217;ve seen thinks that Jay&#8217;s intentions and motives are other than quite respectable and worthy ones.</p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>
				</div>
		<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment odd alt depth-4" id="comment-70657">
				<div id="div-comment-70657" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">joe</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-70657">
			August 28, 2013 at 5:02 am</a>		</div>

		<p>It&#8217;s not a touchy conscience or any such psychological issues &#8212; it&#8217;s being touchy towards what may be &#8220;concern trolling&#8221;. Look above to find some examples of that kind of gamesmanship.</p>
<p>Of course, that&#8217;s one of the evils of concern trolling &#8212; that it creates that kind of touchiness, which then derails good-faith arguments. In the end, open discussion is destroyed by the bad players.</p>
<p>It&#8217;s a very significant issue for democratic polities that is much underdiscussed.</p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>
				</div>
		</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
		<li class="comment even depth-3 parent" id="comment-68970">
				<div id="div-comment-68970" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">Michiel Jonker</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-68970">
			August 17, 2013 at 2:02 pm</a>		</div>

		<p>For the most part, I can follow your interpretation of what &#8220;the banality of evil&#8221; means. One point though: the banality in question does not only manifest itself in the JUSTIFICATION of evil, but also in the evil ITSELF. Possibly, you point to the same thing when you write: &#8220;&#8230;pragmatism as a cover for a great INNER IMMORALITY&#8221; [emphasis mine].</p>
<p>So what is this evil, this inner immorality that can be great and banal at the same time? And what are the &#8220;roots&#8221; of it? I think the roots of it are small and often banal, and I recognize them also in myself, and even in small children. Ever seen a two-year-old grabbing the toy of another two-year-old and pushing him away? There&#8217;s your root of evil.</p>
<p>Roots of evil are important, because by identifying them early on, we can sometimes prevent great evil from happening.</p>
<p>You mention Jay&#8217;s rhetoric and then you state: &#8220;He’s not doing some great evil that requires this many lines of analysis. (&#8230;) Important moral choices are being made. This isn’t one of them.&#8221;</p>
<p>I&#8217;m not going to duplicate the discussion we are having further up on this same thread. I agree with you that Jay is not doing some great evil. But I still think that he makes an important moral choice. The choice to distance himself, as a journalist, from Snowden&#8217;s moral view of things, by disclaiming any position in relation to it. In some cases, &#8220;not making a choice&#8221; means that one also makes a choice. This is such a case.</p>
<p>The choice that Jay makes with his disclaimer (not being &#8220;for&#8221; or &#8220;against&#8221; Snowden) is a choice for a beginning (a &#8220;root&#8221;) of indifference. Keeping a door open for an opt-out if things get really difficult. Indifference, in its turn, can be a root of evil. The indifference of many people in Europe about the fate of those who were persecuted by the Nazis (Jews, other ethnic or cultural groups, homosexuals, critical people) enabled the Nazis to do what they did. The indifference was partly born from fear, and it led to people looking away, not wanting to see what was happening.</p>
<p>Small choices can have great moral significance. I&#8217;m convinced that Keith Alexander and Barack Obama made many small immoral choices, which gradually solidified them into very dishonest men.</p>
<p>Now about Jay&#8217;s choice. There is a difference between, on the one hand, Jay Rosen and almost all other journalists, and on the other hand, Snowden, Greenwald and a few other whistleblowers and journalists. This is NOT the difference between &#8220;the Fourth Estate as a state of mind&#8221; (see the latest blog on this site) and the state of mind of institutionalized journalists and employees. It is clear Jay belongs to this &#8220;Fourth Estate&#8221; at this moment.</p>
<p>The choice of Jay that I have criticized, concerns a difference WITHIN &#8220;the Fourth Estate&#8221;: the difference between those who jump into the water and swim, and those who stay on the riverbank and throw lifejackets at the swimmers.</p>
<p>Both functions are necessary and valuable. But choosing whether to swim or not, is an important moral choice. Without swimmers, the child (a &#8220;well-informed public&#8221;, democracy, rule of law) will drown.</p>
<p>I&#8217;m not one of the swimmers anymore. I&#8217;m trying to climb back on the land. I&#8217;ve learned some lessons about my limitations as a swimmer. And of course no-one can swim forever. Still, I&#8217;m proud that I was a swimmer for some time. And I&#8217;m happy to see the actions of a magnificent swimmer like Snowden. I hope that people in Jay&#8217;s position will have the courage to say publicly: &#8220;Go, Snowden, go! It&#8217;s good what you have done, and what you are doing!&#8221; I don&#8217;t expect Jay to swim. But he might at least step into the water, letting it play around his ankles.</p>
<p>Regarding your last remark: I totally agree with you that the people whom you call &#8220;rational market players&#8221;, for instance in the guise of &#8220;consumers&#8221; who are indifferent to the damage caused by their collective consumption patterns, manifest one form of &#8220;the banality of evil&#8221;.</p>
<p>However, they ARE moral entities. They just don&#8217;t want to make the right choices &#8211; yet.</p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>
				</div>
		<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment odd alt depth-4 parent" id="comment-69722">
				<div id="div-comment-69722" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">Michiel Jonker</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-69722">
			August 22, 2013 at 11:06 am</a>		</div>

		<p>P.S. I just read Jay&#8217;s latest post, &#8220;Conspiracy to commit journalism&#8221; (20 August). There, Jay contrasts &#8220;sunlight coalitions&#8221; defending transparency and public information with authoritarian, secretive &#8220;surveillance-over-everything_forces&#8221;.</p>
<p>I must say, in this later post Jay comes close to &#8220;letting the water play around his ankles&#8221;, because he mentiones &#8220;sources&#8221; as one category of people (along with journalists, couriers, readers who write comments etc.) who form part of these &#8220;sunlight coalitions&#8221;. It is clear that he uses &#8220;sources&#8221; as a code-word, not only for official sources (government spokespeople etc.), but also for whistleblowers, leakers of information etc. So he clearly values the contribution of these last people to transparency, public information and accountability of governments.</p>
<p>However, Jay still does not explicitly mention &#8220;whistleblowers&#8221; (like Snowden) as members of the &#8220;sunlight coalitions&#8221; who fight for saving democracy. Why this strange reluctance?</p>

		<div class="reply">
					</div>
				</div>
		<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment even depth-5" id="comment-70262">
				<div id="div-comment-70262" class="comment-body">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
						<cite class="fn">Michiel Jonker</cite> <span class="says">says:</span>		</div>
		
		<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata"><a href="http://pressthink.org/2013/08/the-toobin-principle/#comment-70262">
			August 26, 2013 at 4:22 pm</a>		</div>

		<p>26 August: new post from Jay Rosen, describing how the global surveillance state tries: &#8220;To make journalism harder, slower, less secure&#8221;</p>
<p>Last sentence: &#8220;Journalism almost has to be brought closer to activism to stand a chance of prevailing in its current struggle with the state.&#8221;</p>
<p>Yes! Now Jay comes (&#8220;almost&#8221;) very close to explicit approval of Snowden&#8217;s moral choice and action. Touching the water with the sole of his foot&#8230;</p>
<p>What Snowden did, can be regarded as part of the work of a very, very brave undercover journalist: penetrating a powerful organisation that had turned criminal, exposing its legal façade for what it is: a façade.</p>

		<div class="reply">

			<div class="textwidget"><p>"We need to keep the press from being absorbed into The Media. This means keeping the word press, which is antiquated. But included under its modern umbrella should be all who do the serious work in journalism, regardless of the technology used. The people who will invent the next press in America--and who are doing it now online--continue an experiment at least 250 years old. It has a powerful social history and political legend attached..." <a href="/about/">Read more</a></p>

You don’t own the press, which is now divided into pro and amateur zones. You don’t control production on the new platform, which isn’t one-way. There’s a new balance of power between you and us.”</p><a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2006/06/27/ppl_frmr.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>

Can we take the quote marks off now? Can we remove the ‘so-called’ from in front? With video!.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2008/07/14/a_most_useful_d.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2003/09/08/basics_master.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">The Master Narrative in Journalism:</a><p>“Were ‘winning’ to somehow be removed or retired as the operating system for news, campaign reporting would immediately become harder to do, not because there would be no news, but rather no common, repeatable instructions for deciding what is a key development in the story, a turning point, a surprise, a trend. Master narratives are thus harder to alter than they are to apprehend. For how do you keep the story running while a switch is made?”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2003/09/08/basics_master.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2009/04/12/hesaid_shesaid.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">He Said, She Said Journalism: Lame Formula in the Land of the Active User</a><p>“Any good blogger, competing journalist or alert press critic can spot and publicize false balance and the lame acceptance of fact-free spin. Do users really want to be left helpless in sorting out who’s faking it more? The he said, she said form says they do, but I say decline has set in.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2009/04/12/hesaid_shesaid.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2006/06/22/blgrc_iv.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Users-Know-More-than-We-Do Journalism:</a><p>“It’s a “put up or shut up” moment for open source methods in public interest reporting. Can we take good ideas like… distributed knowledge, social networks, collaborative editing, the wisdom of crowds, citizen journalism, pro-am reporting… and put them to work to break news?”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2006/06/22/blgrc_iv.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2006/07/25/nadn_qa.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Introducing NewAssignment.Net:</a><p>“Enterprise reporting goes pro-am. Assignments are open sourced. They begin online. Reporters working with smart users and blogging editors get the story the pack wouldn’t, couldn’t or didn’t.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2006/07/25/nadn_qa.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2008/09/18/because_we_have.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">What I Learned from Assignment Zero</a><p>“Here are my coordinates for the territory we need to be searching. I got them from doing a distributed trend story with Wired.com and thinking through the results.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2007/10/09/what_i_learned.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2008/09/18/because_we_have.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">If Bloggers Had No Ethics Blogging Would Have Failed, But it Didn’t. So Let’s Get a Clue:</a><p>“Those in journalism who want to bring ethics to blogging ought to start with why people trust (some) bloggers, not with an ethics template made for a prior platform operating as a closed system in a one-to-many world.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2008/09/18/because_we_have.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2003/09/18/jennings.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">The View From Nowhere:</a><p>“Occupy the reasonable middle between two markers for ‘vocal critic,’ and critics look ridiculous charging you with bias. Their symmetrical existence feels like proof of an underlying hysteria. Their mutually incompatible charges seem to cancel each other out. The minute evidence they marshall even shows a touch of fanaticism.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2003/09/18/jennings.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2005/07/16/rll_back.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Rollback:</a><p>“This White House doesn’t settle for managing the news–what used to be called ‘feeding the beast’–because there is a larger aim: to roll back the press as a player within the executive branch, to make it less important in running the White House and governing the country.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2005/07/16/rll_back.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2006/12/18/suskind_empiricism.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Retreat from Empiricism: On Ron Suskind’s Scoop:</a><p>“”Realist, a classic term in foreign policy debates, and reality-based, which is not a classic term but more of an instant classic, are different ideas. We shouldn’t fuzz them up. The press is capable of doing that because it never came to terms with what Suskind reported in 2004.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2006/12/18/suskind_empiricism.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2007/08/14/rove_and_press.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Karl Rove and the Religion of the Washington Press:</a><p>“Savviness–that quality of being shrewd, practical, well-informed, perceptive, ironic, ‘with it,’ and unsentimental in all things political–is, in a sense, their professional religion. They make a cult of it. And it was this cult that Karl Rove understood and exploited for political gain.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2007/08/14/rove_and_press.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/01/07/press_religion.html#morel" target="_blank" class="highlights">Journalism Is Itself a Religion:</a><p>“We’re headed, I think, for schism, tumult and divide as the religion of the American press meets the upheavals in global politics and public media that are well underway. Changing around us are the terms on which authority can be established by journalists. The Net is opening things up, shifting the power to publish around. Consumers are becoming producers, readers can be writers.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/01/07/press_religion.html#morel" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/12/29/tp04_lctr.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">News Turns from a Lecture to a Conversation:</a><p>“Some of the pressure the blogs are putting on journalists shows up, then, in the demand for “news as conversation,” more of a back-and-forth, less of a pronouncement. This is an idea with long roots in academic journalism that suddenly (as in this year) jumped the track to become part of the news industry’s internal dialogue.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/12/29/tp04_lctr.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2005/03/29/nwsp_dwn.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Laying the Newspaper Gently Down to Die:</a><p>“An industry that won’t move until it is certain of days as good as its golden past is effectively dead, from a strategic point of view. Besides, there is an alternative if you don’t have the faith or will or courage needed to accept reality and deal. The alternative is to drive the property to a profitable demise.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2005/03/29/nwsp_dwn.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2005/12/09/wdwd_grk.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Grokking Woodward:</a><p>“Woodward and Bernstein of 1972-74 didn’t have such access, and this probably influenced–for the better–their view of what Nixon and his men were capable of. Watergate wasn’t broken by reporters who had entree to the inner corridors of power. It was two guys on the Metro Desk.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2005/12/09/wdwd_grk.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2003/10/24/bias_questions.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Maybe Media Bias Has Become a Dumb Debate:</a><p>“This here is a post for practically everyone in the game of seizing on media bias and denouncing it, which is part of our popular culture, and of course a loud part of our politics. And this is especially for the ‘we’re fair and balanced, you’re not’ crowd, wherever I may have located you.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2003/10/24/bias_questions.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2003/10/21/oreilly_voice.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Bill O’Reilly and the Paranoid Style in News:</a><p>“O’Reilly feeds off his own resentments–the establishment sneering at Inside Edition–and like Howard Beale, the ‘mad prophet of the airwaves,’ his resentments are enlarged by the medium into public grievances among a mass of Americans unfairly denied voice.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2003/10/21/oreilly_voice.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2003/12/24/sander_speech.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Thoughts on the Killing of a Young Correspondent:</a><p>“Among foreign correspondents, there is a phrase: ‘parachuting in.’ That’s when a reporter drops into foreign territory during an emergency, without much preparation, staying only as long as the story remains big. The high profile people who might parachute in are called Bigfoots in the jargon of network news. The problem with being a Bigfoot, of course, is that it’s hard to walk in other people’s shoes.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2003/12/24/sander_speech.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2005/03/01/svd_kpln.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">The Abyss of Observation Alone.</a><p>“There are hidden moral hazards in the ethic of neutral observation and the belief in a professional ‘role’ that transcends other loyalties. I think there is an abyss to observation alone. And I feel it has something to do with why more people don’t trust journalists. They don’t trust that abyss.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2005/03/01/svd_kpln.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2007/07/31/newinf_bus.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">“Find Some New Information and Put it Into Your Post.” Standards for Pro-Am Journalism at OffTheBus:</a><p>“Opinion based on information ‘everyone’ has is less valuable than opinion journalism based on information that you dug up, originated, or pieced together. So it’s not important to us that contributors keep opinion&nbsp;<em>out</em>. What’s important is that they put new information&nbsp;<em>in</em>.&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2007/07/31/newinf_bus.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2005/09/29/msm_blgr.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Some Bloggers Meet the Bosses From Big Media:</a><p>“What capacity for product development do news organizations show? Zip. How are they on nurturing innovation? Terrible. Is there an entreprenurial spirit in newsrooms? No. Do smart young people ever come in and overturn everything? Never.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2005/09/29/msm_blgr.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/03/08/weblog_demos.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">The Weblog: An Extremely Democratic Form in Journalism</a><p>“It’s pirate radio, legalized; it’s public access coming closer to life. Inside the borders of Blogistan (a real place with all the problems of a real place) we’re closer to a vision of ‘producer democracy’ than we are to any of the consumerist views that long ago took hold in the mass media, including much of the journalism presented on that platform.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/03/08/weblog_demos.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/03/25/con_prep.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">No One Owns Journalism:</a><p>“And Big Media doesn’t entirely own the press, because if it did then the First Amendment, which mentions the press, would belong to Big Media. And it doesn’t. These things were always true. The weblog doesn’t change them. It just opens up an outlet to the sea. Which in turn extends ‘the press’ to the desk in the bedroom of the suburban mom, where she blogs at night.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/03/25/con_prep.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/01/03/inside_baseball.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Horse Race Now! Horse Race Tomorrow! Horse Race Forever!:</a><p>“How is it you know you’re the press? Because you have a pass that says PRESS, and people open the gate. The locker room doors admit you.&nbsp;<em>The story must be inside that gate; that’s why they give us credentials. We get closer. We tell the fans what’s going on.</em> And if this was your logic, Bill James tried to bust it. Fellahs, said he to the baseball press, you have to realize that you<em>are</em> the gate.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/01/03/inside_baseball.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/01/23/press_player.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Psst…. The Press is a Player:</a><p>“The answer, I think, involves an open secret in political journalism that has been recognized for at least 20 years. But it is never dealt with, probably because the costs of facing it head on seem larger than the light tax on honesty any open secret demands. The secret is this: pssst… the press is a player in the campaign. And even though it knows this, as everyone knows it, the professional code of the journalist contains no instructions in what the press could or should be playing for?”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/01/23/press_player.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/03/20/die_strategy.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Die, Strategy News:</a><p>“I think it’s a bankrupt form. It serves no clear purpose, has no sensible rationale. The journalists who offer us strategy news do not know what public service they are providing, why they are providing it, for whom it is intended, or how we are supposed to use this strange variety of news.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/03/20/die_strategy.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/06/04/ruten_milbank.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">He Said, She Said, We Said:</a><p>“When journalists avoid drawing open conclusions, they are more vulnerable to charges of covert bias, of having a concealed agenda, of not being up front about their perspective, of unfairly building a case (for, against) while pretending only to report ‘what happened.’”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/06/04/ruten_milbank.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/07/16/revealer_forum.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">If Religion Writers Rode the Campaign Bus:</a><p>“Maybe irony, backstage peaking and “de-mystify the process” only get you so far, and past that point they explain nothing. Puzzling through the convention story, because I’m heading right into it myself, made me to realize that journalism’s contempt for ritual was deeply involved here. Ritual is newsless; therefore it must be meaningless. But is that really true?.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/07/16/revealer_forum.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/09/22/gour_talk.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Philip Gourevitch: Campaign Reporting as Foreign Beat:</a><p>“‘A presidential election is a like a gigantic moving television show,’ he said. It is the extreme opposite of an overlooked event. The show takes place inside a bubble, which is a security perimeter overseen by the Secret Service. If you go outside the bubble for any reason, you become a security risk until you are screened again by hand.”<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/09/22/gour_talk.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/02/23/gopnik_time.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">What Time is it in Political Journalism?</a><p>“Adam Gopnik argued ten years ago that the press did not know who it was within politics, or what it stood for. There was a vacuum in journalism where political argument and imagination should be. Now there are signs that this absence of thought is ending.”</p>&nbsp;<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/02/23/gopnik_time.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
				<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/02/29/off_grid.html" target="_blank" class="highlights">Off the Grid Journalism:</a><p>“The assignment was straightforward enough,” writes Marjie Lundstrom of the Sacramento Bee, “talk to people.” When a writer dissents from it or departs from it, the master narrative is a very real thing. Here are two examples: one from politics, one from music.<a href="http://archive.pressthink.org/2004/02/29/off_grid.html" target="_blank" class="highlights-more">Read more</a>
